To find $f(y) + g(y)$, we simply add the two polynomial expressions: $$f(y) + g(y) = (y^4 -3y^3 +y - 3) + (y^3 + 7y^2 -2).$$Combining like terms yields: \begin{align*}
f(y) + g(y) &= y^4 + (-3y^3 + y^3) + (7y^2) + (y-2-3) \\
&= \boxed{y^4 - 2y^3 + 7y^2 + y - 5}.
\end{align*}

Applying $f^{-1}$ to both sides of the equation $f(f(x)) = x$, we get $f^{-1}(f(f(x))) = f^{-1}(x)$.  By definition of the inverse function, $f^{-1}(f(x)) = x$, so $f^{-1}(f(f(x))) = f(x)$.  Then $f(x) = f^{-1}(x)$, so $f(x) - f^{-1}(x) = \boxed{0}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
Since $f(f(x))=x$, we know that $f$ is its own inverse.  In other words, $f(f^{-1}(x))=x$ for all $x$.  Therefore, $$f(x)-f^{-1}(x) = f(f(f(x))) - f(f(x)) = x - f(f(x)).$$ But we are given that $f(f(x))=x$, so $f(x)-f^{-1}(x) = x - x = \boxed{0}$.

Since the points $(-1,0)$ and $(5,0)$ have the same $y$-value, the axis of symmetry of the parabola must be between these 2 points.  The $x$-value halfway between $-1$ and $5$ is $x=2$.  Therefore the vertex of the parabola is equal to $(2,k)$ for some $k$ and the parabola may also be written as  \[a(x-2)^2+k.\] Now we substitute.  The point $(5,0)$ gives  \[0=a(5-2)^2+k,\] or  \[9a+k=0.\] The point $(0,5)$ gives  \[5=a(0-2)^2+k\] or  \[4a+k=5.\] Subtracting the second equation from the first gives  \[(9a+k)-(4a+k)=0-5\] so $5a=-5$, giving $a=-1$.

Since $a=-1$ and $9a+k=0$ we know that $k=9$ and our parabola is  \[ax^2+bx+c=-(x-2)^2+9.\] In order to compute $100a+10b+c$ we can substitute $x=10$ and that gives  \[100a+10b+c=-(10-2)^2+9=\boxed{-55}.\]

Extracting Answer from: 
Substitute $-1$ into the quadratic $ax^2+bx+c$ to obtain $a(-1)^2 + b(-1) + c = a - b + c = 0$, since $(-1,0)$ lies on the parabola.  Thus $a+b = c$.

Substitute $0$ into the quadratic $ax^2+bx+c$ to get $a(0)^2 + b(0) + c = c= 5$, since $(0,5)$ lies on the parabola.

Substitute $5$ into the quadratic $ax^2+bx+c$ to get $a(5)^2 + b(5) + c = 25a + 5b + c = 0$, since $(5,0)$ lies on the parabola.

Thus we have the equations \begin{align*}
a + b &= 5 \\
c &= 5 \\
25a + 5b + c &= 0.
\end{align*} Multiplying the first equation by $25$ and subtracting from the third equation, we get $20a - 120 = 0$, so $a=6$.  Substituting into $a+b=5$, we find $b = -1$.  Therefore $c = 5$, and $$100a+10b+c = 100(6) + 10(-1) + 5 = \boxed{595}.$$

GPT File: datasets/4-20-total500qs/algebra/answers/1210_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
We can form this arithmetic sequence by starting with 13 and repeatedly adding 7. Given the first term 13, common difference 7, and last term 2008, we seek the number of terms in this sequence. Let $n$ be the number of terms. Then the last term is equal to the first term plus the sum of all the rest: \[2008 = 13 + 7 + 14 + \dots + (7n-14) + (7n-7) = 13 + 7(1+2+\cdots+(n-1)+(n-2)+(n-1)).\]Equivalently, we have $7(1+2+\cdots+(n-1)+(n-2)+(n-1)) = 1995$. We try the smallest $n$ until we get a sum/divisor that works: $n=13$ gives the sum $1+2+\cdots+12 = 78$, and $7 \cdot 78 = 546$, which is not divisible by 5.

$n=14$ gives the sum $1+2+\cdots+13 = 91$, and $7 \cdot 91 = 637$, which is divisible by 5. We can see that the other options aren't realistic so $\boxed{14}$ integers belong to the sequence.

Extracting Answer from: 
Notice that the second equation factors as $(2x+1)(2x+3)=0$. This means that $x=-\frac{1}{2},-\frac{3}{2}$ are solutions to the second equation. However, we only need the value(s) of $x$ that satisfy both equations. 

Let's use the quadratic formula to solve the first equation. We have $a=18$, $b=25$, and $c=-3$, so 
\begin{align*}
x &=\frac{-b\pm\sqrt{b^2-4ac}}{2a} \\
&= \frac{-25\pm\sqrt{25^2-4\cdot 18\cdot (-3)}}{2\cdot 18}\\
&= \frac{-25\pm\sqrt{949}}{36}.
\end{align*} 

So, the possible values of $x$ are $\frac{-25+\sqrt{949}}{36}$ and $\frac{-25-\sqrt{949}}{36}$. We need to check each of these values to see if they satisfy the second equation. 

Checking $x=\frac{-25+\sqrt{949}}{36}$: 

\begin{align*}
4\left(\frac{-25+\sqrt{949}}{36}\right)^2+8\left(\frac{-25+\sqrt{949}}{36}\right)+3 &= \frac{1}{81}\left[32\sqrt{949}-879\right] \\
&\neq 0.
\end{align*}

Checking $x=\frac{-25-\sqrt{949}}{36}$:

\begin{align*}
4\left(\frac{-25-\sqrt{949}}{36}\right)^2+8\left(\frac{-25-\sqrt{949}}{36}\right)+3 &= \frac{1}{81}\left[-32\sqrt{949}-879\right] \\
&\neq 0.
\end{align*}

So, neither of the possible values for $x$ satisfies the second equation. Therefore, there is no solution to the system of equations.

By the definition of inverse proportion, the product $jk$ is always equal to some constant $C$.  Substituting the given values, we can see that $42\cdot 56= 2352=C$.  Using this $C$ value, we can solve for $j$ when $k=32$: \begin{align*}
j\cdot 32&=2352\\
\Rightarrow\qquad j&=\frac{2352}{32}=\boxed{73.5}
\end{align*}

We first find the $x$ values at which the two equations intersect. When $x\ge 0$, $y=|x|=x$. Plugging this into the second equation to eliminate $y$, we get $x=-x^2+\frac{35}{4}\Rightarrow x^2+x-\frac{35}{4}=0$. Factoring the left hand side gives $\left(x+\frac{7}{2}\right)\left(x-\frac{5}{2}\right)=0$, so $x=2.5$ (since we stated that $x$ was non-negative). By symmetry, the $x$ value of the left intersection is $x=-2.5$. So we just have to consider the integer $x$ values between these two bounds and find all integer $y$ values that make the point $(x,y)$ fall inside the region.

For $x=-2$, the value of $y=|x|$ is $y=2$ and the value of $y=-x^2+\frac{35}{4}$ is $y=\frac{19}{4}=4.75$, so all $y$ values between 2 and 4 inclusive work, for a total of 3 points. For $x=-1$, the value of $y=|x|$ is $y=1$ and the value of $y=-x^2+\frac{35}{4}$ is $y=\frac{31}{4}=7.75$, so all $y$ values between 1 and 7 inclusive work, for a total of 7 points. For $x=0$, the value of $y=|x|$ is $y=0$ and the value of $y=-x^2+\frac{35}{4}$ is $y=\frac{35}{4}=8.75$, so all $y$ values between 0 and 8 inclusive work, for a total of 9 points. By symmetry, when $x=1$, there are 7 points that work, and when $x=2$, there are 3 points that work.

In total, there are $3+7+9+7+3=\boxed{29}$ lattice points in the region or on the boundary.

Since $y=|x|$ and $y=-x^2+\frac{35}{4}$ intersect at $(\frac{5}{2},\frac{5}{2})$ and $(-\frac{5}{2},\frac{5}{2})$, respectively, we can find the lattice points bounded by the quadrilateral whose vertices are $(\pm 2,2)$ and $(\frac{5}{2},\frac{5}{2})$.

[asy]

Clearly, $(0,2)$ and $(\frac{5}{2},\frac{5}{2})$ are lattice points, and so are all the points on the ends of the horizontal line segment joining them.  We can also travel two steps to the left from $(0,2)$ to get to $(-2,2)$, and two steps to the right to get to $(2,2)$.  Finally, every point on the edges of the quadrilateral $0\leq x\leq 2$, $x\leq y\leq 2$, and $y\leq -x^2+\frac{35}{4}$ are all lattice points as well.  Thus there are $4+2+3+3=\boxed{12}$ total lattice points in the region.

Adding the first given equation to the second, we have $a+2b+c=5$. Then, subtracting the third given equation from this last equation, we get $2b=10$, so $b=5$. Plugging this value of $b$ into the first given equation, we find that $a=3$. Plugging this value of $a$ into the third given equation, we find that $c=-8$. Thus, the product $abc=3\cdot5\cdot-8=\boxed{-120}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
One way to approach this problem is to use algebra. We can start by adding the three equations together: 

$$ (a + b) + (b + c) + (a + c) = 8 - 3 - 5 $$ 

which simplifies to 

$$ 2a + 2b + 2c = 0 $$ 

Dividing both sides by 2, we get 

$$ a + b + c = 0 $$ 

Now we can use this equation to solve for one of the variables in terms of the other two. For example, solving for $c$, we get 

$$ c = -a - b $$ 

Substituting this expression for $c$ into the second equation, we have 

$$ b + (-a - b) = -3 $$ 

which simplifies to 

$$ a = -3 $$ 

Using this value of $a$ in the third equation, we get 

$$ -3 + c = -5 $$ 

which gives us 

$$ c = -2 $$ 

Finally, substituting these values of $a$ and $c$ into the equation $a + b + c = 0$, we have 

$$ -3 + b - 2 = 0 $$ 

which implies 

$$ b = 5 $$ 

Therefore, $a=-3$, $b=5$, and $c=-2$, and the product $abc$ is

Extracting Answer from: 
Let the other two vertices of the square be $(x_1,y_1)$ and $(x_2,y_2)$. 

Since the opposite sides of a square are parallel and of equal length, we know that the difference in $x$-coordinates between $(1,-2)$ and $(-4,10)$ must be the same as the difference in $y$-coordinates. That is,

$$(x_1-1)-(x_2+4) = (-2-y_1)-(10-y_2).$$

Simplifying, we get $x_1-x_2-y_1+y_2=-17$. Since the other two sides of the square are also of length $d$, we have

$$d^2 = (x_1-x_2)^2 + (y_1-y_2)^2.$$

But we also know that $d^2 = (1+4)^2 + (-2-10)^2 = 225$, so we can write

$$(x_1-x_2)^2 + (y_1-y_2)^2 = 225.$$

Now we have two equations in two variables:

\begin{align*}
x_1-x_2-y_1+y_2 &= -17 \\
(x_1-x_2)^2 + (y_1-y_2)^2 &= 225
\end{align*}

We can solve for $x_1,x_2,y_1,y_2$ using these equations (there are different ways to do this, such as substitution or elimination). One possible solution is:

$$x_1 = 4, \quad x_2 = -3, \quad y_1 = 8, \quad y_2 = -4.$$

(Note that we could also have swapped $y_1$ and $y_2$ to get another valid solution.) 

The distance between $(1,-2)$ and $(4,8)$ is $\sqrt{(4-1)^2+(-2-8)^2} = \sqrt{90}$, so the perimeter of the square is $4\sqrt{90} = \boxed{12\sqrt{10}}$.

We can pair up the first and last terms: $1+100=101$. We can then pair up the second and second-to-last terms: $2+99=101$. This pattern continues, giving us $3+98=101$, $4+97=101$, and so on. We can pair up all the numbers like this, until we have 50 pairs of numbers all equaling 101. Therefore, their sum is $50\cdot 101 = \boxed{5050}$.

The points of intersection of the line $y = 7$ and $y = 2x^2 + 8x + 4$, by substitution, are found when $2x^2 + 8x + 4 = 7 \Longrightarrow 2x^2 + 8x - 3 = 0$. By the quadratic formula, $$x = \frac{-8 \pm \sqrt{8^2 - 4 \cdot 2 \cdot (-3)}}{2 \cdot 2}.$$We want to find the difference of these roots to find the difference of the x-coordinates of the point of intersection, which will give a side length of the square. The difference is given by $\frac{\sqrt{8^2 - 4 \cdot 2 \cdot (-3)}}2 = \frac{\sqrt{88}}{2} = \sqrt{22}$. Thus, the area of the square is $\boxed{22}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
Since the side of the square lies on the line $y=7,$ we know that its length is $7.$ Let the two endpoints of this side be $(a,7)$ and $(b,7),$ where $a$ and $b$ are real numbers and $b>a.$ Then the other two vertices of the square must lie on the parabola $y=2x^2+8x+4.$

[asy]
size(150);
import TrigMacros;

real g(real x) { return 2*x*x + 8*x + 4; }
pair A = (-3,7), B = (1,7);
real a = -2, b = 0.74123;
pair C = (a,g(a)), D = (b,g(b));
draw(A--B, red);
draw(reflect((0,0),(0,1))*(A--B), red);
draw(C--D--(D.y,A.x)--(C.y,A.x)--C);
draw(graph(g,-4,2.5), Arrows(4));
draw((-4,0)--(2.5,0),Arrows(4));
draw((0,-3)--(0,17),Arrows(4));
label("$y=2x^2+8x+4$",(2.5,g(2.5)),E);
label("$y=7$",(2.5,7),E);
label("$A$",A,NW); label("$B$",B,NE);
label("$C$",C,SE); label("$D$",D,SW);
dot((0,0));
[/asy]

Let the other two vertices have coordinates $(x,2x^2+8x+4)$ and $(y,2y^2+8y+4).$ Since this is a square, the side lengths are all equal. Hence the length of this side must be $|y-x|.$ Therefore we have the equations
\begin{align*}
|y-x| &= 7 \\
2x^2 + 8x + 4 &= 7 \\
2y^2 + 8y + 4 &= 7.
\end{align*}
The first equation gives $y = x+7$ or $y = x-7.$ We plug in both of these possibilities into the second equation, getting
\begin{align*}
2x^2 + 8x + 4 &= 7 \\
\implies 2x^2 + 8x - 3 &= 0,
\end{align*}
or
\begin{align*}
2(x-7)^2 + 8(x-7) + 4 &= 7 \\
\implies 2(x-7)^2 + 8(x-7) - 3 &= 0.
\end{align*}
Using the quadratic formula, the first equation has solutions
\[
x = \frac{-8\pm\sqrt{8^2-4(2)(-3)}}{4}
= \frac{-2\pm\sqrt{7}}{2}
= -1\pm\frac{\sqrt7}2,
\]
and the second equation has solutions
\begin{align*}
x &= \frac{-8\pm\sqrt{8^2-4(2)(-3)}}{4} + 14 \\
&= \frac{-2\pm\sqrt{7}}{2} + 14 \\
&= 13\pm\frac{\sqrt7}2.
\end{align*}
Thus we have four possibilities for $(x,y):$
\[(-1+\tfrac{\sqrt7}2,6+\tfrac{\sqrt7}2),(13-\tfrac{\sqrt7}2,20-\tfrac{\sqrt7}2),(-1-\tfrac{\sqrt7}2,6-\tfrac{\sqrt7}2),(13+\tfrac{\sqrt7}2,20+\tfrac{\sqrt7}2).\]
We check that exactly two of these pairs of points satisfy the equation $|y-x|=7.$ Those two pairs are $(-1+\tfrac{\sqrt7}2,6+\tfrac{\sqrt7}2)$ and $(13-\tfrac{\sqrt7}2,20-\tfrac{\sqrt7}2).$ Their difference in $y$-coordinates is $\frac{\sqrt7}2 + 13\sqrt7 - \frac{\sqrt7}2 - 7\sqrt7 = 6\sqrt7,$ so the area of the square is $(6\sqrt7)^2 = \boxed{252}.$

\begin{align*}

&= 2 \left[ \left( t - \frac{5}{4} \right)^2 - \frac{5^2}{4^2} \right] + 29 \\

\end{align*}Thus, the minimum height is $\frac{207}{8}.$  To the nearest integer, this is $\boxed{26}.$

We notice that $2t^2-5t+29$ is a quadratic expression that is always increasing. Therefore, if Ben is trying to minimize his height, he should choose the smallest possible value of $t$. The $t$-coordinate of the vertex of $2t^2-5t+29$ is $t = -\frac{-5}{2\cdot 2} = \frac{5}{4}$, which is the value of $t$ where his height is at its minimum.

Plugging in $t=\frac{5}{4}$, we find Ben's minimum height to be $$2\cdot \left(\frac{5}{4}\right)^2 - 5\cdot\frac{5}{4} + 29 = \frac{41}{8} \approx 5.125.$$To the nearest foot, his minimum height will be $\boxed{5}$ feet off the ground.

By the Pythagorean theorem, the distance from $(2,n)$ to $(-1,1)$ is $\sqrt{(2-(-1))^2+(n-1)^2}$.  Setting this equal to $5$, we find \begin{align*}
9+(n-1)^2 &= 25 \implies \\
(n-1)^2 &= 16 \implies \\
n-1 = 4 \quad&\text{or}\quad n-1=-4 \implies \\
n = 5 \quad&\text{or}\quad n=-3.
\end{align*} Both of these solutions are integers, and their product is $\boxed{-15}$.

The distance between $(2,n)$ and $(-1,1)$ is $\sqrt{(2-(-1))^2 + (n-1)^2} = \sqrt{9 + (n-1)^2}$. This is equal to 5, so we have $\sqrt{9 + (n-1)^2} = 5$. Squaring both sides gives $9 + (n-1)^2 = 25$, or $(n-1)^2 = 16$. Thus, $n-1$ is $\pm 4$, so $n$ is either 5 or -3. The product of these values is $5 \cdot (-3) = \boxed{-15}$.

We focus on the portion of the floor that is left unpainted. After one day, $\frac12$ of the floor is left unpainted. After two days, $\frac1{2^2}$ of the floor is left unpainted, and so on. After $n$ days, $\frac1{2^n}$ of the floor is unpainted. The floor has area $15^2 = 225$ square feet, so we look for the least number of days $n$ when at most $\frac1{225}$ of the floor is left unpainted: \begin{align*}
\frac1{2^n} &\leq \frac1{225}\\
\Rightarrow 2^n &\geq 225\\
\Rightarrow n&\geq8.
\end{align*} Thus, Zeno took $\boxed{8}$ days to paint the floor.

If the given quadratic equation has one solution, it follows that its discriminant must be equal to $0$. The discriminant of the given quadratic is given by $m^2 - 4(m+n)$, and setting this equal to zero, we obtain another quadratic equation $m^2 - 4m - 4n = 0$. Since the value of $m$ is unique, it follows that again, the discriminant of this quadratic must be equal to zero. The discriminant is now $4^2 - 4(-4n) = 16 + 16n = 0$, so it follows that $n = \boxed{-1}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
If the quadratic equation has one real root, then the discriminant must be equal to zero. Therefore,

$$
m^2 - 4(m+n) = 0
$$

Solving for $n$, we get:

$$
n = \frac{m^2}{4} - m = \frac{m(m-4)}{4}
$$

Since $m$ is a unique value, $m-4 \neq 0$, and therefore $n$ is uniquely determined based on the value of $m$.

As the center of the circle is at the point $(-5,2)$ and its radius is $r$, the equation for the circle is $(x+5)^2+(y-2)^2=r^2$. Expanding this, \begin{align*}
x^2+10x+25+y^2-4y+4 &= r^2 \\
x^2 + y^2+10x-4y &= r^2-29.
\end{align*}Now, this equation must match the form $Ax^2 + 2y^2 + Bx + Cy = 40,$ so we see that we can multiply the above by two so that the coefficients for $y^2$ match: $$2x^2 + 2y^2+20x-8y= 2r^2-58.$$Thus, $A=2$, $B=20$, and $C=-8$. Also, $2r^2-58=40 \Rightarrow 2r^2=98 \Rightarrow r^2=49$. As $r$ is the radius, it must be positive, so $r=7$.
Therefore, $A+B+C+r= 2+20-8+7= \boxed{21}$.

If $101 \le x \le 110$, then notice that $10 = \sqrt{100} < \sqrt{x}< 11 = \sqrt{121}$. Thus, $\lfloor \sqrt{x} \rfloor^2 = 10^2 = 100$. The desired sum is then $(101 - 100) + (102 - 100) + \cdots + (110 - 100) = 1 + 2 + \cdots + 10 = \frac{10 \cdot 11}{2} = \boxed{55}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
For any integer $n$,
\[n^2 \le 101 \le n^2 + (2n + 1).\]Therefore, $n \le \sqrt{101} < n + 1,$ or in other words, $\lfloor \sqrt{101} \rfloor = 10.$

For any integer $n$,
\[(n + 1)^2 > n^2 + (2n + 1) \ge n^2 + 2n,\]so
\[n + \frac{1}{2} \le \sqrt{n^2 + (2n + 1)} < n + 1.\]Thus,
\[\lfloor \sqrt{101} \rfloor^2 + 1 \le 102 \le (10 + 1)^2,\]so $\lfloor \sqrt{102} \rfloor = 10.$

For any integer $n$,
\[(n + 1)^2 > n^2 + (2n + 2) \ge n^2 + 2n + 1,\]so
\[n + \frac{4}{2} \le \sqrt{n^2 + (2n + 2)} < n + 1.\]Thus,
\[(10 + 1)^2 \le 103 \le 11^2,\]so $\lfloor \sqrt{103} \rfloor = 10.$

Similarly, for any integer $n,$
\[(n + 1)^2 > n^2 + (2n + 3) \ge n^2 + 2n + 1,\]so
\[n + \frac{5}{2} \le \sqrt{n^2 + (2n + 3)} < n + 1.\]Hence,
\[11^2 \le 104 \le 11^2 + 1,\]so $\lfloor \sqrt{104} \rfloor = 11.$

For any integer $n,$
\[(n + 1)^2 > n^2 + (2n + 4) \ge n^2 + 2n + 1,\]so
\[n + \frac{6}{2} \le \sqrt{n^2 + (2n + 4)} < n + 1.\]Therefore,
\[11^2 + 1 \le 105 \le (11 + 1)^2,\]so $\lfloor \sqrt{105} \rfloor = 10.$

For any integer $n,$
\[(n + 1)^2 > n^2 + (2n + 5) \ge n^2 + 2n + 1,\]so
\[n + \frac{7}{2} \le \sqrt{n^2 + (2n + 5)} < n + 1.\]Thus,
\[(11 + 1)^2 \le 106 \le 12^2,\]so $\lfloor \sqrt{106} \rfloor = 10.$

Similarly, we compute that $\lfloor \sqrt{107} \rfloor = \lfloor \sqrt{108} \rfloor = \lfloor \sqrt{109} \rfloor = \lfloor \sqrt{110} \rfloor = 10.$

Therefore,
\begin{align*}
f(101) + f(102) + f(103) + \dots + f(110) &= \left( 101 - 10^2 \right) + \left( 102 - 10^2 \right) + \dots + \left( 110 - 10^2 \right) \\
&= 101 + 102 + \dots + 110 - 10 \cdot 10 \\
&= \boxed{465}.

The absolute value of the product of two numbers is the product of their absolute values, so we can write  \[
|x^2-16|=|(x+4)(x-4)|=|x+4|\,|x-4|.
\]Since $|x^2-16|$ is written as the product of two positive integers, it is composite unless one of the integers is $1$.  Solving $|x+4|=1$, we observe that either $x+4=1$ or $x+4=-1$, which give solutions of $x=-3$ and $x=-5$.  Similarly, solving $|x-4|=1$ we find $x=3$ or $x=5$. Among the possible solutions $\{-5,-3,3,5\}$, only $\{-3,3\}$ yield a prime value for $|x+4|\,|x-4|$.  Therefore, the product of the integer values of $x$ for which $|x^2-16|$ is a prime number is $\boxed{-9}$.

We will have $|x^2 - 16|=p$ or $|x^2-16|=-p$ for some prime number $p$. Let's handle these cases separately.

Case 1: $|x^2-16|=p$

If $x^2-16$ is positive, then we have $x^2-16=p$. Adding 16 to both sides yields $x^2=p+16$, so $x$ must be a positive or negative integer if $p+16$ is a perfect square. Since $p$ is prime, $p+16$ is even for $p>2$, so $p+16$ is a perfect square and we can write $p+16=k^2$ for some positive integer $k$. Rearranging gives $p=k^2-16$. Since $p$ is prime, $k^2-16$ must be a prime, so $k^2-16$ must be equal to either 2 or an odd prime. If $k^2-16=2$, then $k^2=18$, which does not give an integer solution for $k$. If $k^2-16$ is an odd prime, then $k$ must be even (since $k^2-16$ is even), so we can write $k=2m$ for some positive integer $m$, which gives $p=k^2-16=(2m)^2-16=4m^2-16=4(m^2-4)$. Since $p$ is prime, this means $m^2-4=1$ or $m^2-4=-1$, which only has solutions $m=3$ and $m=1$, respectively. Thus, $k=6$ or $k=2$, giving $p=k^2-16=20$ or $p=0$, respectively.

If $x^2-16$ is negative, then we have $|x^2-16|=p$. Taking the negative of both sides yields $x^2-16=-p$, so $x$ is a positive or negative integer if $p-16$ is a perfect square. Since $p$ is prime and $p>16$, $p-16$ is also prime and we can write $p-16=k^2$ for some positive integer $k$. Rearranging gives $p=k^2+16$. Since $p$ is prime, $k^2+16$ must be a prime, so $k^2+16$ must be equal to an odd prime. $k$ cannot be odd (since $k^2$ must be even), so $k$ must be even and we can write $k=2m$ for some positive integer $m$. This gives $p=k^2+16=(2m)^2+16=4m^2+16=4(m^2+4)$. Since $p$ is prime, this means $m^2+4=1$, which has no integer solutions for $m$. Thus, there are no solutions in this case.

Case 2: $|x^2-16|=-p$

If $x^2-16$ is positive, then we have $|x^2-16|=-p$. Taking the negative of both sides yields $x^2-16=p$, which is the same as Case 1, so we already know the solutions in this case.

If $x^2-16$ is negative, then we have $|x^2-16|=-p$. Taking the negative of both sides yields $x^2-16=-p$, which is the same as Case 1. However, we know from the condition $|x^2-16|=-p$ that $p$ is negative, so we need to look for negative values of $p$ in Case 1. There is only one such value, $p=0$, which gives $x=4$ or $x=-4$.

Thus, the possible values of $x$ are $2$, $-2$, $4$, and $-4$, and the product of these values is $2\cdot(-2)\cdot4\cdot(-4)=\boxed{-64}$.

First we bring all the $x$ and $y$ terms to the left, and put all the other terms on the right. If the system has infinitel solutions, it means that the two equations are equivalent, which in turn means they are scalar multiples of each other. We can check this by seeing if we can manipulate one equation to get the other. Starting with $3x + 2y = 8$, we can get $y$ on one side: $y = \frac{8 - 3x}{2}$. Substituting this into the second equation, we get $6x = 2a - 7 - 4\left(\frac{8 - 3x}{2}\right)$. Simplifying, we have $6x = -4x + (2a - 7 + 16)$. Combining like terms and solving for $a$, we get $a = \boxed{9}$.

Let $a = x-d,$ $b = x-c,$ and $c = x-b$ for some $x.$ Then, $a,b,d$ form a geometric progression, so $b^2 = ad.$ Since $b = x-c$ and $c = x-b,$ we have $c = x-(x-c) = c$ for all $x.$ That is, $b$ and $d$ are equidistant from $c,$ so $d = c + (c-b) = 2c-b.$

Then $a = x-d = x - (2c-b) = b - c,$ so $a+b = 2b-c.$ But $a,b,c,d$ form an increasing arithmetic progression, so $2b = a+d.$ Therefore, $$b^2 = ad = bd - b^2 \implies bd = 2b^2.$$Since $b = x-c$ and $d = 2c-b,$ we can write $bd = (x-c)(2x-3c).$ After expanding, we get \begin{align*}
2(x-c)^2 &= (x-c)(2x-3c) \\
2(x-c) &= 2x - 3c \\
x &= 2c - (x-c).
\end{align*}Then, we have $a = b - c = (x-c)-c = x-2c,$ and $d = 2c - b = 2c - (x-c) = 3c-x.$

Finally,\[\frac ad = \frac{x-2c}{3c-x}.\]To find this in terms of $b$ only, we can substitute $x = 2c - (x-c) = 3c-b$ and obtain \[\frac ad = \frac{b-c}{c-b} = \boxed{-1}.\]

Using the quadratic formula, we find that the roots of the quadratic are $\frac{-7\pm\sqrt{7^2-4(4)(k)}}{8}=\frac{-7\pm\sqrt{49-16k}}{8}$. Since the problem tells us that these roots must equal $\frac{-7\pm\sqrt{15}i}{8}$, \begin{align*} \sqrt{49-16k}&=\sqrt{15}i
\\\Rightarrow\qquad \sqrt{49-16k}&=\sqrt{-15}
\\\Rightarrow\qquad 49-16k&=-15
\\\Rightarrow\qquad 16k&=64
\\\Rightarrow\qquad k&=\boxed{4}.
\end{align*}

Since the quadratic has complex roots $\frac{-7\pm i\sqrt{15}}{8}$, we know that the discriminant of the quadratic, $b^2-4ac$, is negative. Therefore, we have:

\begin{align*}
b^2-4ac &= 7^2 - 4(4)(k) < 0\\
49-16k &<0 \\
16k &> 49 \\
k &> \frac{49}{16}
\end{align*}

Thus, the smallest possible value of $k$ is $\frac{49}{16}$, and any larger value will still give us complex roots. Therefore, we can say that $k\geq \frac{49}{16}$.

We have \begin{align*} (2x+10)(x+3)&<(3x+9)(x+8) \quad \Rightarrow
\\ 2(x+5)(x+3)&<3(x+3)(x+8) \quad \Rightarrow
\\ 2(x+5)(x+3)-3(x+3)(x+8)&<0 \quad \Rightarrow
\\ (2x+10-(3x+24))(x+3)&<0 \quad \Rightarrow
\\ (-x-14)(x+3)&<0 \quad \Rightarrow
\\ (x+14)(x+3)&>0.
\end{align*} This inequality is satisfied if and only if $(x+14)$ and $(x+3)$ are either both positive or both negative.  Both factors are positive for $x>-3$ and both factors are negative for $x<-14$.  When $-14<x<-3$, one factor is positive and the other negative, so their product is negative.   Therefore, the range of $x$ that satisfies the inequality is $ \boxed{(-\infty, -14)\cup(-3,\infty)} $.

Extracting Answer from: 
Expanding both sides of the inequality gives $2x^2 + 16x + 30 < 3x^2 + 33x + 72$. Simplifying and moving all the terms to one side gives $x^2 + 17x + 42 < 0$. Factoring this quadratic gives $(x+14)(x+3) < 0$. The product of these two factors is negative when $-14 < x < -3$. Therefore, the solution to the inequality is $-14 < x < -3$, or in interval notation, $\boxed{(-14,-3)}$.

We begin by recognizing that since $27 = 3^3$, $7\sqrt{27}$ simplifies to $7\cdot3\sqrt{3}=21\sqrt{3}$. Our expression then becomes  \begin{align*}
\frac{8}{3\sqrt{3}+21\sqrt{3}} & = \frac{8}{24\sqrt{3}} \\
& = \frac{1}{3\sqrt{3}} \\
& = \boxed{\frac{\sqrt{3}}{9}}.
\end{align*}

First, we simplify the denominator by factoring out $\sqrt{3}$ from both terms: $$3\sqrt{3}+ 7\sqrt{27} = 3\sqrt{3} + 7\sqrt{3^3} = 3\sqrt{3} + 7(3\sqrt{3}) = 24\sqrt{3}.$$ 

So, the fraction becomes: $$\frac{8}{3\sqrt{3}+ 7\sqrt{27}} = \frac{8}{24\sqrt{3}}.$$ 

Next, we simplify the fraction by dividing both the numerator and denominator by 8: $$\frac{8}{24\sqrt{3}} = \frac{1}{3\sqrt{3}}.$$ 

Therefore, the simplified expression is $\boxed{\frac{1}{3\sqrt{3}}}$.

Since these equations look symmetric, at least on the left-hand side, we add all three together.  This gives us  \begin{align*}
&a(x+1+y)+b(y+x+1)+c(1+y+x)\\
&\qquad\qquad=x+7+2x+6y+4x+y.
\end{align*} We then factor out $(1+x+y)$ on the left and simplify the right side to get \begin{align*}
(1+x+y)(a+b+c)&=7x+7y+7, \text{ so}\\
(1+x+y)(a+b+c)&=7(1+x+y).
\end{align*}

Since $x+y\neq -1$, we know $x+y+1\neq 0$, so we can divide both sides by that term and find that $a+b+c=\boxed{7}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
Adding the three equations, we get \begin{align*}
(ax+a)+(bx+by)+(cx+cy)+b+c&=7x+5x+5y\\
\Rightarrow (a+b+c)+ (a+b)x+(c+b)y&=12x+5y.
\end{align*}Since $x+y\neq -1$, we can divide both sides by $x+y$ to get  $$a+b+c + (a+b)\left(-1-\dfrac{y}{x+y}\right)=12\dfrac{x}{x+y}-5.$$Recall that $x+y\neq -1$, so $\dfrac{y}{x+y}\neq -1$, and therefore we can manipulate our first equation to get $$\dfrac{x+7-by-c}{a}=x\Rightarrow x+7-by-c=ax.$$ Substituting, we get \begin{align*}
a+b+c - (a+b)\dfrac{y-b+c}{x+y} &=12\dfrac{x}{x+y}-5\\
\dfrac{a+b+c(x+y)-(a+b)(y-b+c)}{x+y}&=12\dfrac{x}{x+y}-5\\
\dfrac{(a+b)2x+(a+b+c)(x+y)-(a+b)(y-b+c)}{x+y}&=12\dfrac{x}{x+y}-5\\
\dfrac{(a+b)(x+2y)+(a+b+c)y-(a+b)c}{x+y} &= 12\dfrac{x}{x+y}-5.
\end{align*}It now remains to simplify the numerator of the left-hand side. We rearrange the third given equation to get $y=4x-(ay+cx)/b$. Substituting this into the second equation gives  \begin{align*}
a+(bx+cy)+c(4x-(ay+cx)/b)&=2x+6y\\
a+\left(b+4c-\dfrac{c^2}{b}\right)x+\left(\dfrac{c^2}{b}-ac\right)y&=6y-4c x.
\end{align*}Therefore, $$a+b+4c-\dfrac{c^2}{b}=0 \hspace{5mm}\text{ and } \hspace{5mm} \dfrac{c^2}{b}-ac=6.$$Hence,$$b=\dfrac{c^2}{6(a+c)}.$$Substituting this value of $b$ into the first equation gives $$ax+c(7-3c/(a+c))=x.$$Rearranging yields $$x=\dfrac{7a+2c}{3c+a}.$$Substituting our expressions for $x$ and $b$ into our expression for $y$ and simplifying, we find that $y=(5a+4c)/(3c+a)$. The numerator of the left-hand side expression for $a+b+c$ then becomes \begin{align*}
&(a+b)(x+2y)+(a+b+c)y-(a+b)c \\
&= \left(a+\dfrac{c^2}{6(a+c)}\right)\left(\dfrac{7a+2c}{3c+a}\right)+2\left(a+\dfrac{c^2}{6(a+c)}\right)\left(\dfrac{5a+4c}{3c+a}\right) \\
&\quad +\left(a+\dfrac{c^2}{6(a+c)}+c\right)\left(\dfrac{5a+4c}{3c+a}\right)-\left(a+\dfrac{c^2}{6(a+c)}+c\right)\left(\dfrac{c^2}{6(a+c)}\right) \\
&= \dfrac{-a^3-8a^2c-12ac^2-4c^3}{3(a+c)^2}.
\end{align*}Therefore, $$a+b+c=\dfrac{-a^3-8a^2c-12ac^2-4c^3}{3(a+c)^2}\cdot\dfrac{(a+c)^2}{ac}=-(a^2+4ac+4c^2)/3.$$

Note that $f(2)=5$ implies $f^{-1}(5)=2$. Applying $f^{-1}(x+4)=2f^{-1}(x)+1$ repeatedly, we have: \begin{align*}
f^{-1}(5)&=2 \\
\Rightarrow \quad f^{-1}(9)&=2f^{-1}(5)+1=5 \\
\Rightarrow \quad f^{-1}(13)&=2f^{-1}(9)+1=11 \\
\Rightarrow \quad f^{-1}(17)&=2f^{-1}(13)+1=23.
\end{align*}So $f^{-1}(17)=\boxed{23}$.

First, we need to manipulate the given functional equation to better understand it. Letting $y = f^{-1}(x)$, we have $f(y) = x$. Substituting this into the given equation, we get $f^{-1}(f(y) + 4) = 2f^{-1}(f(y)) + 1$. Since $f(y) = x$, we can rewrite this as $f^{-1}(x+4) = 2f^{-1}(x) + 1$. Now we can use this equation to find $f^{-1}(17)$:

$$f^{-1}(17+4) = 2f^{-1}(17) + 1$$

$$f^{-1}(21) = 2f^{-1}(17) + 1$$

Letting $a = f^{-1}(17)$, we can rewrite this as $f^{-1}(21) = 2a + 1$. 

Now, we need to find a way to relate $a$ to $f(2)$, which is given. We know that $f(2) = 5$, which means $f^{-1}(5) = 2$. We can use the functional equation to relate $f^{-1}(17)$ to $f^{-1}(5)$:

$$f^{-1}(17+4) = 2f^{-1}(17) + 1$$

$$f^{-1}(21) = 2f^{-1}(17) + 1$$

$$f^{-1}(21) - 1 = 2f^{-1}(17)$$

$$\frac{1}{2}(f^{-1}(21) - 1) = f^{-1}(17)$$

We have expressed $f^{-1}(17)$ in terms of $f^{-1}(21)$, so now we just need to find $f^{-1}(21)$. Substituting $x = 21$ into the equation $f^{-1}(f(y) + 4) = 2f^{-1}(f(y)) + 1$ and using the fact that $f(2) = 5$, we get:

$$f^{-1}(21 + 4) = 2f^{-1}(21) + 1$$

$$f^{-1}(25) = 2f^{-1}(21) + 1$$

Letting $b = f^{-1}(21)$, we can rewrite this as $f^{-1}(25) = 2b + 1$. 

We know that $f$ is the inverse of $f^{-1}$, so we can use $f^{-1}(5) = 2$ to find $f(2)$:

$$f(2) = 5 \Rightarrow f^{-1}(5) = 2$$

$$\Rightarrow f(f^{-1}(5)) = f(2) = 5$$

Now we can use the functional equation to relate $f^{-1}(21)$ to $f^{-1}(5)$:

$$f^{-1}(f(y) + 4) = 2f^{-1}(f(y)) + 1$$

$$f^{-1}(f^{-1}(21) + 4) = 2f^{-1}(f^{-1}(21)) + 1$$

$$f^{-1}(21 + 4) = 2f^{-1}(21) + 1$$

$$f^{-1}(25) = 2f^{-1}(21) + 1$$

$$\Rightarrow f^{-1}(25) = 2b + 1$$

Using the fact that $f^{-1}(5) = 2$:

$$f^{-1}(25) = f^{-1}(f(2) + 4)$$

$$\Rightarrow 2b + 1 = f^{-1}(f(2) + 4)$$

$$\Rightarrow 2b + 1 = f^{-1}(5+4)$$

$$\Rightarrow 2b + 1 = f^{-1}(9)$$

We know that $f^{-1}(9) = f^{-1}(f(4)+4)$:

$$f^{-1}(f(y) + 4) = 2f^{-1}(f(y)) + 1$$

$$f^{-1}(f(4)+4) = 2f^{-1}(f(4)) + 1$$

$$f^{-1}(9) = 2f^{-1}(5) + 1$$

We already know that $f^{-1}(5) = 2$, so we can substitute that in to get:

$$f^{-1}(9) = 2(2) + 1 = 5$$

Substituting this back into the equation we got earlier, we have:

$$2b + 1 = f^{-1}(9) = 5$$

$$2b = 4$$

$$b = 2$$

Therefore, $f^{-1}(17) = \frac{1}{2}(f^{-1}(21) - 1) = \frac{1}{2}(2 - 1) = \boxed{\frac{1}{2}}$.

We make the variable $x$ the number of adult patrons and $y$ the number of children patrons. Since the movie theater is full and seats $100$, $x+y=100$.

Adult tickets sell for $\$9.00$ each, so a total of $9x$ dollars were collected from adults. Children tickets sell for $\$5.00$ each, so a total of $5y$ dollars were collected from children. A total of $\$640$ were collected, so $9x+5y=640$.

We now have two equations, $x+y=100$ and $9x+5y=640$. We now solve for $y$.

We multiply the first equation by $9$ so that we can eliminate the $x$ term: $9x+9y=900$. Then, we subtract the second equation from it to get $9x+9y-(9x+5y)=900-640 \rightarrow 4y=260 \rightarrow y=65$.

Therefore, $\boxed{65 \text{ children tickets }}$ were sold.

Extracting Answer from: 
Let $a$ be the number of adult tickets sold and $c$ be the number of children's tickets sold. We have two equations:

$$\begin{aligned} a+c&=100 \\ 9a+5c&=640 \end{aligned}$$

Multiplying the first equation by $5$ and subtracting it from the second equation multiplied by $9$, we get:

$$9a+5c-5a-5c=640-500$$

Simplifying both sides, we get $4a=140$, so $a=35$. Substituting this back into the first equation, we get $35+c=100$, so $c=65$. Therefore, $65$ children's tickets were sold.

Extracting Answer from: 
Let $p$ be the momentum of the particle and $\Delta p$ be the error in the measurement of its momentum. Similarly, let $x$ be the position of the particle and $\Delta x$ be the error in the measurement of its position. Then Heisenberg's Uncertainty Principle tells us that:

$$\Delta p \cdot \Delta x \geq \frac{\hbar}{2}$$

where $\hbar$ is Planck's constant divided by $2\pi$. We want to find the percentage increase in $\Delta x$ if $\Delta p$ is halved. 

If $\Delta p$ is halved, then the minimum value of $\Delta x$ must increase. Let $\Delta x'$ be the new minimum value. Then we have:

$$\frac{\Delta p}{2} \cdot \Delta x' \geq \frac{\hbar}{2}$$

Simplifying this inequality gives:

$$\Delta x' \geq \frac{\hbar}{\Delta p}\cdot 2 = \frac{2\hbar}{\Delta p}$$

Now we can compare $\Delta x'$ to the original minimum value of $\Delta x$. Let's call this original value $\Delta x_0$. Using Heisenberg's Uncertainty Principle and the fact that $\Delta p \cdot \Delta x_0 = \frac{\hbar}{2}$, we get:

$$\Delta x_0 = \frac{\hbar}{\Delta p}$$

So the percentage increase in $\Delta x$ is:

$$\frac{\Delta x' - \Delta x_0}{\Delta x_0} \cdot 100\% = \frac{\frac{2\hbar}{\Delta p} - \frac{\hbar}{\Delta p}}{\frac{\hbar}{\Delta p}} \cdot 100\% = \boxed{100\%}$$

Therefore, the minimum error in the measurement of the particle's position increases by $100\%$ when the error in the measurement of the particle's momentum is halved.

Since $4,a,b$ is a geometric sequence, we know that $a/4=b/a$ and hence $a^2=4b$. Since $b,c,5$ is an arithmetic sequence, we know that $c-b=5-c$. We now play around with the value of $a$. Next, we see that we need $4,a,b$ to be a geometric sequence, and hence $4,b,a^2$ must be an arithmetic sequence. Thus, $2b = a^2+4$. GPT File: datasets/4-20-total500qs/algebra/answers/26_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
We begin with the first circle, which has center $(24,32)$. Completing the square, we can write this equation as $(x-24)^2 + (y-32)^2 = 32^2$. Similarly, we can write the second equation as $(x+24)^2 + (y+32)^2 = 32^2$. These are two circles with radius $32$, centered at $(24,32)$ and $(-24,-32)$ respectively. 

Let $P$ and $Q$ be the two points of intersection of the two circles. We can see that the line passing through the centers of the two circles, namely $y = x$, bisects the segment $PQ$. To see this, note that this line passes through the midpoint of the segment connecting the centers of the two circles, which is the origin. Therefore, the distance $d$ between the two circles is twice the distance between the center of one of the circles and the point where this line intersects the circle. 

[asy]
unitsize(0.2cm);
import graph;
pair A=(24,32);
pair B=(-24,-32);
real f(real x)
{

return x;
};
xaxis(Label("$x$"),-55, 55, Ticks(" ",1.0));
yaxis(Label("$y$"),-55,55,Ticks(" ",1.0));
draw(Circle(A,32));
draw(Circle(B,32));
draw((-55,-55)--(55,55),red);
dot(Label("$P$"),intersectionpoints(Circle(A,32),Circle(B,32))[0],UnFill);
dot(Label("$Q$"),intersectionpoints(Circle(A,32),Circle(B,32))[1],UnFill);
label("$O_1$",A,N);
label("$O_2$",B,SW);
draw((55,55)--(-55,-55),red);
label("$y=x$",(55,55),NE);
[/asy]

We can find the intersection points $P$ and $Q$ by solving the two equations $(x-24)^2 + (y-32)^2 = 32^2$ and $(x+24)^2 + (y+32)^2 = 32^2$. Subtracting these equations, we get \[\begin{aligned} (x-24)^2 - (x+24)^2 + (y-32)^2 - (y+32)^2 &= 0 \\ -48x - 64y &= 0 \\ x + \frac{4}{3}y &= 0 \end{aligned}\] Solving this system with either of the two original equations, we get $x = -\frac{48}{5}$ and $y = \frac{36}{5}$ (the other solution is $x = \frac{48}{5}$ and $y = -\frac{36}{5}$, which is the reflection of the first solution across the line $y = x$). Therefore, the distance $d$ between the two circles is $d = 2\sqrt{(24 + 48/5)^2+(32 - 36/5)^2-32^2} = \boxed{\frac{144}{5}}$.

First, we factor the denominator, to get \[\frac{x^2+5x+\alpha}{x^2 + 7x - 44} = \frac{x^2 + 5x + \alpha}{(x - 4)(x + 11)}.\]If this fraction can be expressed as a quotient of two linear functions, then the numerator must have either a factor of $x - 4$ or $x + 11$.

If the numerator has a factor of $x - 4$, then by the factor theorem, it must be 0 when $x = 4$.  Hence, $4^2 + 5 \cdot 4 + \alpha = 0$, which means $\alpha = -36$.

If the numerator has a factor of $x + 11$, then it must be 0 when $x = -11$.  Hence, $(-11)^2 + 5 \cdot (-11) + \alpha = 0$, which means $\alpha = -66$.

Therefore, the sum of all possible values of $\alpha$ is $-36 + (-66) = \boxed{-102}$.

Let $p(x) = x^2 + 5x + \alpha$ and $q(x) = x^2 + 7x - 44$, and suppose $\frac{p(x)}{q(x)}$ can be expressed as $\frac{ax+b}{cx+d}$ for some real numbers $a,b,c,d$ with $c,d$ not both equal to $0.$ Then we have \[ax^2 + bx + cx + d = (ax+b)(cx+d) = p(x) = x^2 + 5x + \alpha,\]so \[(ac)x^2 + (ad+bc)x + bd = x^2 + 5x + \alpha.\]Since these polynomials are equal for all $x,$ we must have $ac = 1,$ $ad+bc = 5,$ and $bd = \alpha.$

Since $c,d$ are not both $0,$ we may assume without loss of generality that $c \neq 0.$ Then $a = \frac{1}{c},$ and $ad+bc=5$ becomes $d + b/c = 5.$

We can solve for $b$ and $d$ in terms of $c$ and $a$ by writing $d = 5 - b/c$ and substituting into $bd = \alpha$ to get \[b(5-b/c) = \alpha,\]which rearranges to $b^2/c - 5b/c + \alpha = 0.$ By the quadratic formula, we get \[b = \frac{5c \pm \sqrt{25c^2 - 4c^2\alpha}}{2c} = \frac{5 \pm \sqrt{25 - 4\alpha}}{2} c. \hspace{1cm} (\spadesuit)\]If $25 - 4\alpha < 0,$ then the denominator of $b$ in $(\spadesuit)$ is nonzero only if $c$ is negative, so we may assume that $c < 0.$ But then $a = \tfrac{1}{c}$ is also negative, so $p(-6)$ is nonpositive, while $q(-6)$ is always positive, contradiction. Therefore, we must have $25-4\alpha \ge 0,$ from which $\alpha \le \tfrac{25}{4}.$

If $25 - 4\alpha > 0,$ then we get two possible values of $b$ in $(\spadesuit).$ In this case, we require that $p(x)/q(x)$ can be expressed as both $\frac{ax+b}{cx+d}$ and $\frac{ax+b'}{cx+d'}$ for some $b \neq b',$ so $b,c,d$ and $b',c,d'$ must all satisfy \[b/c = b'/c \quad \text{and} \quad d+b/c = d'+b'/c.\]The first equation tells us that $b=b',$ and the second equation becomes $d-d' = b/c-b'/c = 0.$ Thus, we must have $b=b'$ and $d=d'.$

We can use the rule we derived above, $b = \frac{5 \pm \sqrt{25 - 4\alpha}}{2} c,$ to determine which values of $c$ admit this. We need both $b$ and $d$ to be nonzero, so we must have $(5 + \sqrt{25 - 4\alpha})/2 \neq 0$ and $(5 - \sqrt{25 - 4\alpha})/2 \neq 0.$ These are equivalent to $\alpha \neq 0$ and $\alpha \neq 25,$ respectively.

If $\alpha \in (0,25/4],$ then $(5 + \sqrt{25 - 4\alpha})/2$ and $(5 - \sqrt{25 - 4\alpha})/2$ are nonzero and of opposite sign, so we can choose $c>0$ to make them both positive or $c<0$ to make them both negative. Letting $k = \frac{5 + \sqrt{25 - 4\alpha}}{2c},$ we have $b = kc$ and $d = 5 - k,$ so we get \[\frac{x^2+5x+\alpha}{x^2+7x-44} = \frac{\frac{1}{c}x+kc}{x+5-kc}\]for all nonzero $x.$ Since this expression is valid for all $x$ except for $x=-5+kc,$ we must have $-5+kc = -4 \pm 2\sqrt{6},$ which gives two possible values of $\alpha,$ namely $\alpha_1 = \frac{165 - 30\sqrt{6}}{4} < \frac{25}{4}$ and $\alpha_2 = \frac{165 + 30\sqrt{6}}{4} > 25/4.$ However, $\alpha_2$ is not in the range $(0,25/4],$ so we discard it. Thus, we obtain the unique solution $\alpha = \alpha_1,$ and we can check that $\alpha_1$ works, so $S = \{ \alpha_1 \},$ and the sum of the elements of $S$ is $\boxed{\frac{165-30\sqrt{6}}{4}}.$

If $\alpha = 0$ (resp. $\alpha = 25$), we instead get $b=0$ (resp. $d=0$). In either case, it is easy to check that there is no value of $c$ that works, so $0$ and $25$ are not in $S.$

&=\frac{1}{2000}\cdot\frac{k}{E}\\
&=\frac{1}{2\times10^3}\cdot7\times10^{-7}\\

\end{align*} Therefore, we have $a+b = \boxed{-6.5}$.

Since energy is inversely proportional to wavelength, the energy of a photon of red light is multiplied by a factor of $2000$ to obtain the energy of the other photon. Since $E = hc/\lambda$, where $E$ is energy, $h$ is Planck's constant, $c$ is the speed of light, and $\lambda$ is wavelength, we have $$\frac{E_\text{new}}{E_\text{red}} = \frac{\lambda_\text{red}}{\lambda_\text{new}}.$$ Substituting what we know, we get $$\frac{2000}{1} = \frac{7\times 10^{-7}}{\lambda_\text{new}}.$$ Solving for $\lambda_\text{new}$, we get $\lambda_\text{new} = \frac{7\times 10^{-7}}{2000}$. Writing this in scientific notation gives $a = 3.5\times 10^{-10}$ and $b = -0.5$, so $a+b = \boxed{0.35}$.

Expanding the expression $(x+m)^2-63$, we get $x^2+2mx+m^2-63$. Setting this equal to the given quadratic $x^2+bx+1$, we have:

\begin{align*}
x^2+bx+1 &= x^2+2mx+m^2-63 \\
bx &= 2mx+m^2-64 \\
\end{align*}

For this equation to hold for all $x$, the coefficients of $x$ must be equal. So we have:

$$b=2m$$

Now let's focus on the expression in the square, $(x+m)^2-63$. We know that this expression is equivalent to $x^2+bx+1$. Setting these equal to each other, we have:

$$(x+m)^2-63 = x^2+bx+1$$

Expanding, we get:

$$x^2+2mx+m^2-63 = x^2+bx+1$$

Canceling $x^2$ terms and rearranging, we have:

$$(2m-b)x = 62-m^2$$

For this equation to hold for all $x$, the coefficients on both sides of the equation must be equal. We know that the left-hand side is equal to $(2m-b)x$, so we must have:

$$62-m^2 = 0 \quad\Rightarrow\quad m = \sqrt{62} \quad\text{(since } m > 0 \text{)}$$

Now that we know $m$, we can use the fact that $b=2m$ to find $b$:

$$b = 2m = 2\sqrt{62}$$

Therefore, $b = \boxed{2\sqrt{62}}$.

Let the constant term of Charles's quadratic be $c$, and the constant term of Eric's quadratic be $d$. Then Charles's discriminant is $(4)^2-4(1)(c)=16-4c$, and Eric's discriminant is $(4)^2-4(1)(d)=16-4d$. We're given that $$\frac{\text{Discriminant}_{\text{Eric}}}{\text{Discriminant}_{\text{Charles}}}=\frac{\text{Constant}_{\text{Charles}}}{\text{Constant}_{\text{Eric}}},$$or $\frac{16-4d}{16-4c}=\frac{c}{d}$. Cross multiplying gives  \begin{align*}
d(16-4d)&=c(16-4c)\quad\Rightarrow\\
16d-4d^2&=16c-4c^2\quad\Rightarrow\\
4c^2-4d^2&=16c-16d\quad\Rightarrow\\
4(c+d)(c-d)&=16(c-d).
\end{align*}Since $c\neq d$, we know that $c-d\neq 0$, so we can cancel this term to find  \begin{align*}
4(c+d)&=16\quad\Rightarrow\\
c+d&=4.
\end{align*}Thus the sum of Eric's and Charles's constant terms is $\boxed{4}$.

Let Eric's polynomial be $x^2+4x+a$ and Charles's polynomial be $x^2+4x+b$. Then we have $$\frac{(4)^2-4(a)(1)}{(4)^2-4(b)(1)} = \frac{b}{a}.$$ Simplifying, we have $b(a+4) = a(b+4)$, which gives us $ab+4b = a(b+4)$ or $ab+4b=ab+4a$. Subtracting $ab$ from both sides gives $4b=4a$, which implies $a=b$. We now look at the ratio of the constant terms of their polynomials: $$\frac{b}{a} = \frac{a}{a} = 1.$$ Therefore, the constant terms of their polynomials are equal, and the sum of their constant terms is $2a+2b=\boxed{8}$.

Notice that by the logarithmic identity $\log(x) - \log(y) = \log\frac{x}{y}$, the equations are equivalent to $\log\frac{x}{y}=a$, $\log\frac{y}{z}=15$, and $\log\frac{z}{x}=-7$ respectively. Adding all three equations together yields $\log\frac{x}{y} + \log\frac{y}{z} + \log\frac{z}{x} = a + 15 - 7$. From the identity $\log (x) + \log (y) = \log (xy)$, we obtain $\log\left(\frac{x}{y}\cdot\frac{y}{z}\cdot\frac{z}{x}\right) = a + 8$. Cancelations result in $\log(1) = a + 8$. Since $\log(1) = 0$, we find $a = \boxed{-8}$.

We can use the properties of logarithms to simplify these equations: \begin{align*}
\log\frac{x}{y} &= a, \\
\log\frac{y}{z} &= 15, \text{ and} \\
\log\frac{z}{x} &= -7.
\end{align*}We can solve for $z$ in the second equation: $$\log\frac{y}{z}=15 \quad \Rightarrow \quad \frac{y}{z}=10^{15}.$$We can solve for $x$ in the third equation: $$\log\frac{z}{x}=-7 \quad \Rightarrow \quad \frac{z}{x}=10^{-7}.$$Multiplying these two equations, we get $$\frac{y}{z}\cdot\frac{z}{x}=10^{15}\cdot 10^{-7}=10^8.$$Therefore, $$\frac{y}{x}=10^8,$$which we can substitute into the first equation: $$\log\frac{x}{y}=\log\frac{1}{10^8}=-8.$$Thus, $a=\boxed{-8}$.
